<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg"><img alt="Diane de Versailles Leochares.jpg" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="800" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg/220px-Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg/330px-Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg/440px-Diane_de_Versailles_Leochares.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Diana_of_Versailles" title="Diana of Versailles">Diana of Versailles</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman</a> copy of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_in_ancient_Greece" title="Art in ancient Greece">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a> by <a href="/wiki/Leochares" title="Leochares">Leochares</a><br/>
(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>)</div>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border:2px solid white;background:white;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif"><img alt="Laurel wreath fa13.gif" class="noviewer" data-file-height="65" data-file-width="74" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif/16px-Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif/24px-Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif/32px-Laurel_wreath_fa13.gif 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Hellenismos" title="Portal:Hellenismos">Hellenismos portal</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Template:Ancient Greek religion"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Template talk:Ancient Greek religion"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_Greek_religion&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Artemis</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/ primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="/…ëÀêr/ 'ar' in 'far'">…ëÀêr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/…™/ or /…ô/ 'e' in 'roses'">·µª</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/…™/ or /…ô/ 'e' in 'roses'">·µª</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient¬†Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">·ºåœÅœÑŒµŒºŒπœÇ</span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Language" title="Greek Language">Modern Greek</a>: ŒÜœÅœÑŒµŒºŒπœÇ, <i>√Årtemis</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic Greek</a>:¬†</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Greek" title="Help:IPA for Greek">[√°r.te.mis]</a></span>) was one of the most widely venerated of the Ancient Greek deities. Her <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> equivalent is <a href="/wiki/Diana_(mythology)" title="Diana (mythology)">Diana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lar_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lar-1">[1]</a></sup> Some scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SacksMurray2009_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-SacksMurray2009-2">[2]</a></sup> believe that the name, and indeed the goddess herself, was originally pre-Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Homer refers to her as <i>Artemis Agrotera, <a href="/wiki/Potnia_Theron" title="Potnia Theron">Potnia Theron</a></i>: "Artemis of the wildland, Mistress of Animals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">Arcadians</a> believed she was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the classical period of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, Artemis was often described as the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leto" title="Leto">Leto</a>, and the twin sister of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>. She was the Hellenic goddess of the <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wild animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virginity</a> and protector of young girls, bringing and relieving disease in women; she often was depicted as a huntress carrying a bow and arrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cupressus" title="Cupressus">cypress</a> were sacred to her. In later Hellenistic times, she even assumed the ancient role of <a href="/wiki/Eileithyia" title="Eileithyia">Eileithyia</a> in aiding childbirth.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="999" data-file-width="2000" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg/220px-Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg/330px-Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg/440px-Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Didrachme_de_Ionie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Didrachm from <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, representing the goddess Artemis</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="2286" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg/220px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg/330px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1a/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg/440px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_tetradrachm_of_Indo-Greek_king_Artemidoros.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver tetradrachm of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek" title="Indo-Greek">Indo-Greek</a> king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemidoros" title="Artemidoros">Artemidoros</a> (whose name means "gift of Artemis"), c. 85 BCE, featuring Artemis with a drawn bow and a quiver on her back on the reverse of the coin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The name Artemis (<a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun"><i>noun</i></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_(linguistics)" title="Gender (linguistics)"><i>feminine</i></a>) is of unknown or uncertain origin and etymology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Babiniotis_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Babiniotis-8">[8]</a></sup> although various ones have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrew_Lang_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Lang-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_Anthon_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Anthon-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, according to <a href="/wiki/Johann_Theodor_Jablonski" title="Johann Theodor Jablonski">J. T. Jablonski</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_Anthon_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Anthon-10">[10]</a></sup> the name is also <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygian</a> and could be "compared with the royal appellation <i>Artemas</i> of <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Anthon" title="Charles Anthon">Charles Anthon</a> the primitive root of the name is probably of Persian origin from *<i>arta</i>, *<i>art</i>, *<i>arte</i>, all meaning "great, excellent, holy," thus Artemis "becomes identical with the great mother of Nature, even as she was worshipped at Ephesus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_Anthon_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Anthon-10">[10]</a></sup> Anton Goebel "suggests the root œÉœÑœÅŒ±œÑ or ·ø•Œ±œÑ, "to shake," and makes Artemis mean the thrower of the dart or the shooter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrew_Lang_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Lang-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Babiniotis" title="Georgios Babiniotis">Babiniotis</a>, while accepting that the etymology is unknown, states that the name is already attested in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenean_Greek" title="Mycenean Greek">Mycenean Greek</a> and is possibly of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Greek_substrate" title="Pre-Greek substrate">pre-Hellenic</a> origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Babiniotis_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Babiniotis-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name could also be possibly related to <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>√°rktos</i> "<a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a>" (from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">PIE</a> *<i>h‚ÇÇ≈ïÃ•t·∏±os</i>), supported by the bear cult that the goddess had in <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brauronia#Cult_of_Artemis_Brauronia" title="Brauronia">Brauronia</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> remains at the <a href="/wiki/Arkoudiotissa_Cave" title="Arkoudiotissa Cave">Arkoudiotissa Cave</a>, as well as the story about <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(mythology)" title="Callisto (mythology)">Callisto</a>, which was originally about Artemis (<a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">Arcadian</a> epithet <i>kallisto</i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> this cult was a survival of very old totemic and shamanistic rituals and formed part of a larger <a href="/wiki/Bear_worship" title="Bear worship">bear cult</a> found further afield in other <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European</a> cultures (e.g., Gaulish <a href="/wiki/Artio" title="Artio">Artio</a>). It is believed that a precursor of Artemis was worshiped in <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan Crete</a> as the goddess of mountains and hunting, <a href="/wiki/Britomartis" title="Britomartis">Britomartis</a>. While connection with <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> names has been suggested,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> the earliest attested forms of the name <i>Artemis</i> are the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a> <span lang="gmy" xml:lang="gmy">êÄÄêÄ≥êÄñêÄµ</span>, <i>a-te-mi-to</i> /Artemitos/ and <span lang="gmy" xml:lang="gmy">êÄÄêÄ¥êÄñêÄ≥</span>, <i>a-ti-mi-te</i> /Artimitei/, written in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> at <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._P._Beekes" title="Robert S. P. Beekes">R. S. P. Beekes</a> suggested that the <i>e</i>/<i>i</i> interchange points to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Greek" title="Pre-Greek">Pre-Greek</a> origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Artemis was venerated in <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> as <i>Artimus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ancient Greek writers, by way of <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a>, and some modern scholars, have linked Artemis (Doric <i>Artamis</i>) to ·ºÑœÅœÑŒ±ŒºŒøœÇ, <i>artamos</i>, i.e. "butcher"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> or, like <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> did in <i><a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)">Cratylus</a></i>, to <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">·ºÄœÅœÑŒµŒºŒÆœÇ</span>, <i>artem·∏ós</i>, i.e. "safe", "unharmed", "uninjured", "pure", "the stainless maiden".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrew_Lang_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Lang-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_Anthon_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Charles_Anthon-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artemis_in_mythology">Artemis in mythology</span></h2>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p>Leto bore Apollon and Artemis, delighting in arrows,<br/>
Both of lovely shape like none of the heavenly gods,<br/>
As she joined in love to the <a href="/wiki/Aegis" title="Aegis">Aegis</a>-bearing ruler.</p>
</div>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>‚Äî‚Äâ<a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>, lines 918‚Äì920 (written in the 7th century BCE)</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1870" data-file-width="1640" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg/250px-Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg/375px-Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg/500px-Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_Apollo_Louvre_Myr199.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Artemis</i> (on the left, with a deer) and <i>Apollo</i> (on the right, holding a lyre) from <a href="/wiki/Myrina,_Greece" title="Myrina, Greece">Myrina</a>, dating to approximately 25 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2370" data-file-width="2410" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg/250px-Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg/375px-Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg/500px-Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Artemis_Brygos_Louvre_G151.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Apollo (left) and Artemis</i>. <a href="/wiki/Brygos" title="Brygos">Brygos</a> (potter, signed), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Briseis_Painter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Briseis Painter (page does not exist)">Briseis Painter</a>, Tondo of an Attic red-figure cup, ca. 470 BC, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Various conflicting accounts are given in Classical Greek mythology of the birth of Artemis and her twin brother, Apollo. All accounts agree, however, that she was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leto" title="Leto">Leto</a> and that she was the twin sister of Apollo.</p>
<p>An account by <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a> has it that Hera forbade Leto to give birth on either terra firma (the mainland) or on an island. Hera was angry with Zeus, her husband, because he had impregnated Leto. But the island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Ortygia" title="Ortygia">Ortygia</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymn to Artemis</a>) disobeyed Hera, and Leto gave birth there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In ancient Cretan history Leto was worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Phaistos" title="Phaistos">Phaistos</a> and in Cretan mythology Leto gave birth to Apollo and Artemis at the islands known today as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paximadia_(islands)" title="Paximadia (islands)">Paximadia</a>.</p>
<p>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholium" title="Scholium">scholium</a></i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servius" title="Servius">Servius</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> iii. 72 accounts for the island's archaic name Ortygia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> by asserting that Zeus transformed Leto into a <a href="/wiki/Quail" title="Quail">quail</a> (<i>ortux</i>) in order to prevent Hera from finding out his infidelity, and Kenneth McLeish suggested further that in quail form Leto would have given birth with as few birth-pains as a mother quail suffers when it lays an egg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The myths also differ as to whether Artemis was born first, or Apollo. Most stories depict Artemis as born first, becoming her mother's mid-wife upon the birth of her brother Apollo.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2410" data-file-width="1820" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg/250px-Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg/375px-Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg/500px-Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_Kephisodotos_Musei_Capitolini_MC1123.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roman marble <i>Bust of Artemis</i> after <a href="/wiki/Cephisodotus_the_Elder" title="Cephisodotus the Elder">Kephisodotos</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musei_Capitolini" title="Musei Capitolini">Musei Capitolini</a>), Rome.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The childhood of Artemis is not fully related in any surviving myth. The <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> reduced the figure of the dread goddess to that of a girl, who, having been thrashed by <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, climbs weeping into the lap of Zeus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> A poem of <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a> to the goddess "who amuses herself on mountains with archery" imagines some charming vignettes: according to Callimachus, at the age of three years, Artemis, while sitting on the knee of her father, Zeus, asked him to grant her six wishes: to remain always a virgin; to have many names to set her apart from her brother <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>; to be the <b>Phaesporia</b> or Light Bringer; to have a bow and arrow and a knee-length tunic so that she could hunt; to have sixty "daughters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okeanos" title="Okeanos">Okeanos</a>", all nine years of age, to be her choir; and for twenty Amnisides Nymphs as handmaidens to watch her dogs and bow while she rested. She wished for no city dedicated to her, but to rule the mountains, and for the ability to help women in the pains of childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Artemis believed that she had been chosen by <a href="/wiki/Moirai" title="Moirai">the Fates</a> to be a midwife, particularly since she had assisted her mother in the delivery of her twin brother, Apollo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> All of her companions remained virgins, and Artemis closely guarded her own chastity. Her symbols included the golden bow and arrow, the hunting dog, the stag, and the moon. Callimachus tells<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-26">[26]</a></sup> how Artemis spent her girlhood seeking out the things that she would need to be a huntress, how she obtained her bow and arrows from the isle of <a href="/wiki/Lipari" title="Lipari">Lipara</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclops</a> worked.</p>
<p>Okeanus' daughters were filled with fear, but the young Artemis bravely approached and asked for bow and arrows. Callimachus then tells how Artemis visited <a href="/wiki/Pan_(god)" title="Pan (god)">Pan</a>, the god of the forest, who gave her seven bitches and six dogs. She then captured six golden-horned deer to pull her chariot. Artemis practiced with her bow first by shooting at trees and then at wild beasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intimacy">Intimacy</span></h3>
<p>As a virgin, Artemis had interested many gods and men, but only her hunting companion, <a href="/wiki/Orion_(mythology)" title="Orion (mythology)">Orion</a>, won her heart. Orion was accidentally killed either by Artemis or by Gaia.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpheus_(mythology)" title="Alpheus (mythology)">Alpheus</a>, a river god, was in love with Artemis, but he realizes that he can do nothing to win her heart. So he decides to capture her. Artemis, who is with her companions at Letrenoi, goes to Alpheus, but, suspicious of his motives, she covers her face with mud so that the river god does not recognize her. In another story, Alphaeus tries to rape Artemis' attendant <a href="/wiki/Arethusa_(mythology)" title="Arethusa (mythology)">Arethusa</a>. Artemis pities Arethusa and saves her by transforming Arethusa into a spring in Artemis' temple, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemis_Alphaea" title="Artemis Alphaea">Artemis Alphaea</a> in Letrini, where the goddess and her attendant drink.</p>
<p>Bouphagos, the son of the Titan Iapetos, sees Artemis and thinks about raping her. Reading his sinful thoughts, Artemis strikes him at Mount Pholoe.</p>
<p>Sipriotes is a boy, who, either because he accidentally sees Artemis bathing or because he attempts to rape her, is turned into a girl by the goddess.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Actaeon">Actaeon</span></h3>
<p>Multiple versions of the Actaeon myth survive, though many are fragmentary. The details vary but at the core they involve a great hunter, <a href="/wiki/Actaeon" title="Actaeon">Actaeon</a> who Artemis turns into a stag for a transgression and who is then killed by hunting dogs. Usually the dogs are his own, who no longer recognize their master. Sometimes they are Artemis' hounds.</p>
<p>According to the standard modern text on the work, Lamar Ronald Lacey's <i>The Myth of Aktaion: Literary and Iconographic Studies</i>, the most likely original version of the myth is that Actaeon was the hunting companion of the goddess who, seeing her naked in her sacred spring, attempts to force himself on her. For this hubris he is turned into a stag and devoured by his own hounds. However, in some surviving versions Actaeon is a stranger who happens upon her. Different tellings also diverge in the hunter's transgression, which is sometimes merely seeing the virgin goddess naked, sometimes boasting he is a better hunter than she, or even merely being a rival of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> for the affections of <a href="/wiki/Semele" title="Semele">Semele</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adonis">Adonis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg/220px-Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg/330px-Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg/440px-Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giuseppe-Mazzuoli-The-Death-of-Adonis-hermitag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Death of Adonis</i>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Mazzuoli_(1644-1725)" title="Giuseppe Mazzuoli (1644-1725)">Giuseppe Mazzuoli</a>, 1709 - <i><a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In some versions of the story of <a href="/wiki/Adonis" title="Adonis">Adonis</a>, who was a late addition to Greek mythology during the Hellenistic period, Artemis sent a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boar" title="Boar">wild boar</a> to kill Adonis as punishment for his hubristic boast that he was a better hunter than she.</p>
<p>In other versions, Artemis killed Adonis for revenge. In later myths, Adonis had been related as a favorite of <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>, and Aphrodite was responsible for the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippolytus_(mythology)#Hippolytus_as_Virbius" title="Hippolytus (mythology)">Hippolytus</a>, who had been a favorite of Artemis. Therefore, Artemis killed Adonis to avenge Hippolytus‚Äôs death.</p>
<p>In yet another version, Adonis was not killed by Artemis, but by Ares, as punishment for being with Aphrodite.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orion">Orion</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Orion_(mythology)" title="Orion (mythology)">Orion</a> was Artemis' hunting companion. In some versions, he is killed by Artemis, while in others he is killed by a <a href="/wiki/Scorpius#Mythology" title="Scorpius">scorpion</a> sent by <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a>. In some versions, Orion tries to seduce Opis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> one of Artemis' followers, and she kills him. In a version by <a href="/wiki/Aratus" title="Aratus">Aratus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Orion takes hold of Artemis' robe and she kills him in <a href="/wiki/Self-defense" title="Self-defense">self-defense</a>.</p>
<p>In yet another version, Apollo sends the scorpion. According to <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Hyginus" title="Gaius Julius Hyginus">Hyginus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Artemis once loved Orion (in spite of the late source, this version appears to be a rare remnant of her as the pre-Olympian goddess, who took consorts, as <a href="/wiki/Eos" title="Eos">Eos</a> did), but was tricked into killing him by her brother Apollo, who was "protective" of his sister's maidenhood.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Aloadae">The Aloadae</span></h3>
<p>These twin sons of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Iphidemia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Iphidemia (page does not exist)">Iphidemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>, Otos and Ephialtes, grew enormously at a young age. They were aggressive, great hunters, and could not be killed unless they killed each other. The growth of the <a href="/wiki/Aloadae" title="Aloadae">Aloadae</a> never stopped, and they boasted that as soon as they could reach heaven, they would kidnap Artemis and <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> and take them as wives. The gods were afraid of them, except for Artemis who captured a fine deer (or in another version of the story, she changed herself into a doe) and jumped out between them. The Aloadae threw their spears and so mistakenly killed each other.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Callisto">Callisto</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tizian_015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2926" data-file-width="3176" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tizian_015.jpg/220px-Tizian_015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tizian_015.jpg/330px-Tizian_015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tizian_015.jpg/440px-Tizian_015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tizian_015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Diana_and_Callisto" title="Diana and Callisto">Diana and Callisto</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Callisto_(mythology)" title="Callisto (mythology)">Callisto</a> was the daughter of Lycaon, King of Arcadia and also was one of Artemis's hunting attendants. As a companion of Artemis, she took a vow of chastity. Zeus appeared to her disguised as Artemis, or in some stories Apollo, gained her confidence, then took advantage of her (or raped her, according to <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>). As a result of this encounter she conceived a son, Arcas.</p>
<p>Enraged, Hera or Artemis (some accounts say both) changed her into a bear. Arcas almost killed the bear, but Zeus stopped him just in time. Out of pity, Zeus placed Callisto the bear into the heavens, thus the origin of Callisto the Bear as a constellation. Some stories say that he placed both Arcas and Callisto into the heavens as bears, forming the <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Ursa Major</a> constellations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iphigenia_and_the_Taurian_Artemis">Iphigenia and the Taurian Artemis</span></h3>
<p>Artemis punished <a href="/wiki/Agamemnon" title="Agamemnon">Agamemnon</a> after he killed a sacred stag in a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_grove" title="Sacred grove">sacred grove</a> and boasted that he was a better hunter than the goddess. When the Greek fleet was preparing at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aulis_(ancient_greece)" title="Aulis (ancient greece)">Aulis</a> to depart for <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> to begin the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>, Artemis becalmed the winds. The seer <a href="/wiki/Calchas" title="Calchas">Calchas</a> advised Agamemnon that the only way to appease Artemis was to sacrifice his daughter <a href="/wiki/Iphigenia" title="Iphigenia">Iphigenia</a>. Artemis then snatched Iphigenia from the altar and substituted a deer. Various myths have been told around what happened after Artemis took her. Either she was brought to Tauros and led the priests there, or became Artemis' immortal companion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niobe">Niobe</span></h3>
<p>A Queen of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" title="Thebes (Greece)">Thebes</a> and wife of <a href="/wiki/Amphion" title="Amphion">Amphion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niobe" title="Niobe">Niobe</a> boasted of her superiority to Leto because while she had fourteen children (<a href="/wiki/Niobids" title="Niobids">Niobids</a>), seven boys and seven girls, Leto had only one of each. When Artemis and Apollo heard this impiety, Apollo killed her sons as they practiced athletics, and Artemis shot her daughters, who died instantly without a sound. Apollo and Artemis used poisoned arrows to kill them, though according to some versions two of the Niobids were spared, one boy and one girl. Amphion, at the sight of his dead sons, killed himself. A devastated Niobe and her remaining children were turned to stone by Artemis as they wept. The gods themselves entombed them.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chione">Chione</span></h3>
<p>Chione was a princess of Pokis. She was beloved by two gods, <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>, and boasted that she was prettier than Artemis because she made two gods fall in love with her at once. Artemis was furious and killed Chione with her arrow or struck her dumb by shooting off her tongue. However, some versions of this myth say Apollo and Hermes protected her from Artemis' wrath.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atalanta.2C_Oeneus_and_the_Meleagrids">Atalanta, Oeneus and the Meleagrids</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2820" data-file-width="1680" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg/220px-Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg/330px-Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg/440px-Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_libation_Louvre_CA599.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artemis pouring a libation, c. 460-450 BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artemis saved the infant <a href="/wiki/Atalanta" title="Atalanta">Atalanta</a> from dying of exposure after her father abandoned her. She sent a female bear to suckle the baby, who was then raised by hunters. But she later sent a bear to hurt Atalanta because people said Atalanta was a better hunter. This is in some stories.</p>
<p>Among other adventures, Atalanta participated in the hunt for the <a href="/wiki/Calydonian_Boar" title="Calydonian Boar">Calydonian Boar</a>, which Artemis had sent to destroy <a href="/wiki/Calydon" title="Calydon">Calydon</a> because King <a href="/wiki/Oeneus" title="Oeneus">Oeneus</a> had forgotten her at the harvest sacrifices. In the hunt, Atalanta drew the first blood, and was awarded the prize of the skin. She hung it in a sacred grove at <a href="/wiki/Tegea" title="Tegea">Tegea</a> as a dedication to Artemis.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Meleager" title="Meleager">Meleager</a> was a hero of Aetolia. King <a href="/wiki/Oeneus" title="Oeneus">Oeneus</a> had him gather heroes from all over Greece to hunt the <a href="/wiki/Calydonian_Boar" title="Calydonian Boar">Calydonian Boar</a>. After the death of <a href="/wiki/Meleager" title="Meleager">Meleager</a>, Artemis turned his grieving sisters, the <a href="/wiki/Meleagrids" title="Meleagrids">Meleagrids</a> into <a href="/wiki/Guineafowl" title="Guineafowl">guineafowl</a> that Artemis loved very much.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aura">Aura</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Dionysiaca" title="Dionysiaca">Dionysiaca</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aura_(mythology)" title="Aura (mythology)">Aura</a> was Greek goddess of breezes and cool air, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Lelantos" title="Lelantos">Lelantos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periboia" title="Periboia">Periboia</a>. She was a virgin huntress, just like Artemis and proud of her maidenhood. One day, she claimed that the body of Artemis was too womanly and she doubted her virginity. Artemis asked <a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(mythology)" title="Nemesis (mythology)">Nemesis</a> for help to avenge her dignity and caused the rape of Aura by <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>. Aura became a mad and dangerous killer. When she bore twin sons, she ate one of them while the other one, Iakhos, was saved by Artemis. Iakhos later became an attendant of <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> and the leader of <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_Mysteries" title="Eleusinian Mysteries">Eleusinian Mysteries</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polyphonte">Polyphonte</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polyphonte" title="Polyphonte">Polyphonte</a> was a young woman who fled home preferring the idea of a virginal life with Artemis to the conventional life of marriage and children favoured by Aphrodite. As a punishment Aphrodite cursed her, causing her to have children by a bear. The resulting offspring, Agrius and Oreius, were wild cannibals who incurred the hatred of Zeus. Ultimately the whole family were transformed into birds and more specifically ill portents for mankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trojan_War">Trojan War</span></h3>
<p>Artemis may have been represented as a supporter of Troy because her brother <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> was the patron god of the city and she herself was widely worshipped in western Anatolia in historical times. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> she came to blows with Hera, when the divine allies of the Greeks and Trojans engaged each other in conflict. Hera struck Artemis on the ears with her own quiver, causing the arrows to fall out. As Artemis fled crying to Zeus, Leto gathered up the bow and arrows.</p>
<p>Artemis played quite a large part in this war. Like her mother and brother, who was widely worshiped at Troy, Artemis took the side of the Trojans. At the Greek's journey to Troy, Artemis becalmed the sea and stopped the journey until an oracle came and said they could win the goddess' heart by sacrificing <a href="/wiki/Iphigenia" title="Iphigenia">Iphigenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agamemnon" title="Agamemnon">Agamemnon</a>'s daughter. Agamemnon once promised the goddess he would sacrifice the dearest thing to him, which was Iphigenia, but broke the promise. Other sources<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2010)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> said he boasted about his hunting ability and provoked the goddess' anger. Artemis saved Iphigenia because of her bravery. In some versions of the myth,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2010)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> Artemis made Iphigenia her attendant or turned her into <a href="/wiki/Hecate" title="Hecate">Hecate</a>, goddess of night, witchcraft, and the underworld.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> was helped by Artemis, Leto, and Apollo. Apollo found him wounded by Diomedes and lifted him to heaven. There, the three of them secretly healed him in a great chamber.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_of_Artemis">Worship of Artemis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg/220px-Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg/330px-Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg/440px-Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jerash_Temple_of_Artemis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Roman Temple of Artemis</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jerash,_Jordan" title="Jerash, Jordan">Jerash, Jordan</a>, built during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brauronia" title="Brauronia">Brauronia</a></div>
<p>Artemis, the goddess of forests and hills, was worshipped throughout <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Her best known <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">cults</a> were on the island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> (her birthplace), in Attica at <a href="/wiki/Brauron" title="Brauron">Brauron</a> and Mounikhia (near <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a>), and in <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>. She was often depicted in paintings and statues in a forest setting, carrying a bow and arrows, and accompanied by a deer.</p>
<p>The ancient Spartans used to sacrifice to her as one of their patron goddesses before starting a new <a href="/wiki/Military_campaign" title="Military campaign">military campaign</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Athenian_festivals" title="Athenian festivals">Athenian festivals</a> in honor of Artemis included <a href="/wiki/Elaphebolia" title="Elaphebolia">Elaphebolia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mounikhia" title="Mounikhia">Mounikhia</a>, Kharisteria, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brauronia" title="Brauronia">Brauronia</a>. The festival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemis_Orthia" title="Artemis Orthia">Artemis Orthia</a> was observed in <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>.</p>
<p>Pre-pubescent and adolescent Athenian girls were sent to the sanctuary of Artemis at Brauron to serve the Goddess for one year. During this time, the girls were known as <i>arktoi</i>, or little she-bears. A myth explaining this servitude states that a bear had formed the habit of regularly visiting the town of Brauron, and the people there fed it, so that, over time, the bear became tame. A girl teased the bear, and, in some versions of the myth, it killed her, while, in other versions, it clawed out her eyes. Either way, the girl's brothers killed the bear, and Artemis was enraged. She demanded that young girls "act the bear" at her sanctuary in atonement for the bear's death.</p>
<p>Virginal Artemis was worshipped as a fertility/childbirth goddess in some places, assimilating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilithyia" title="Ilithyia">Ilithyia</a>, since, according to some myths, she assisted her mother in the delivery of her twin. During the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical period</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, she was identified with <a href="/wiki/Hecate" title="Hecate">Hecate</a>. Artemis also assimilated <a href="/wiki/Caryatis" title="Caryatis">Caryatis</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carya_(goddess)" title="Carya (goddess)">Carya</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epithets">Epithets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2562" data-file-width="2418" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg/220px-Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg/330px-Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg/440px-Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artemis_found_in_Pompeii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Color reconstruction of a first-century AD statue of Artemis found in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>. Reconstructed using analysis of trace pigments. It was an imitation of Greek statues of the sixth century BC. Part of <a href="/wiki/Gods_in_Color" title="Gods in Color">Gods in Color</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As <b>Aeginaea</b>, she was worshiped in <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>; the name means either huntress of <a href="/wiki/Chamois" title="Chamois">chamois</a>, or the wielder of the javelin (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Œ±·º∞Œ≥Œ±ŒΩŒ≠Œ±</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Also in Sparta, Artemis <i>Lygodesma</i> was worshipped. This epithet means "willow-bound" from the Gr. <i>lygos</i> (ŒªœÖŒ≥œåœÇ, willow) and <i>desmos</i> (Œ¥ŒµœÉŒºœåœÇ, bond). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willow_tree" title="Willow tree">willow tree</a> appears in several ancient Greek myths and rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was worshipped at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naupactus" title="Naupactus">Naupactus</a> as <b>Aetole</b>; in her temple in that town there was a statue of white marble representing her throwing a javelin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This "Aetolian Artemis" would not have been introduced at Naupactus, anciently a place of <a href="/wiki/Ozolian_Locris" title="Ozolian Locris">Ozolian Locris</a>, until it was awarded to the <a href="/wiki/Aetolia" title="Aetolia">Aetolians</a> by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>. Strabo records another precinct of "Aetolian Artemos" at the head of the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Agoraea" title="Agoraea">Agoraea</a> she was the protector of the <a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a>.</p>
<p>As <b><a href="/wiki/Agrotera" title="Agrotera">Agrotera</a></b>, she was especially associated as the patron goddess of hunters. In Athens Artemis was often associated with the local <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aeginian</a> goddess, <b><a href="/wiki/Aphaea" title="Aphaea">Aphaea</a></b>. As <b><a href="/wiki/Potnia_Theron" title="Potnia Theron">Potnia Theron</a></b>, she was the patron of wild animals; <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> used this title. As <b><a href="/wiki/Kourotrophos" title="Kourotrophos">Kourotrophos</a></b>, she was the nurse of youths. As <b>Locheia</b>, she was the goddess of childbirth and midwives.</p>
<p>She was sometimes known as <b><a href="/wiki/Cynthia" title="Cynthia">Cynthia</a></b>, from her birthplace on <a href="/wiki/Cynthus" title="Cynthus">Mount Cynthus</a> on <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a>, or <b>Amarynthia</b> from a festival in her honor originally held at Amarynthus in <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>.</p>
<p>She was sometimes identified by the name <b>Phoebe</b>, the feminine form of her brother Apollo's solar epithet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoebus" title="Phoebus">Phoebus</a>.</p>
<p><b>Alphaea</b>, <b>Alpheaea</b>, or <b>Alpheiusa</b> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Gr.</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">·ºàŒªœÜŒ±·øñŒ±</span>, <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">·ºàŒªœÜŒµŒ±ŒØŒ±</span>, or <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">·ºàŒªœÜŒµŒπŒø·ø¶œÉŒ±</span>) was an epithet that Artemis derived from the river god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpheus_(mythology)" title="Alpheus (mythology)">Alpheius</a>, who was said to have been in love with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DGRBM_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-DGRBM-40">[40]</a></sup> It was under this name that she was worshiped at Letrini in <a href="/wiki/Elis" title="Elis">Elis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Ortygia" title="Ortygia">Ortygia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Artemis Alphaea was associated with the wearing of masks, largely because of the legend that while fleeing the advances of Alpheius, she and her <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">nymphs</a> escaped him by covering their faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>As <b>(Artemis) Anaitis</b>, the 'Persian Artemis' was identified with <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Apanchomene" title="Apanchomene">Apanchomene</a>, she was worshipped as a hanged goddess.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brauron-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Brauron-2.jpg/220px-Brauron-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Brauron-2.jpg/330px-Brauron-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Brauron-2.jpg/440px-Brauron-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brauron-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sanctuary of Artemis at <a href="/wiki/Brauron" title="Brauron">Brauron</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artemis was born on the sixth day, which made it sacred for her.</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brauronia" title="Brauronia">Festival of Artemis in Brauron</a>, where girls, aged between five and ten, dressed in <a href="/wiki/Saffron_(color)" title="Saffron (color)">saffron</a> robes and played at being bears, or "act the bear" to appease the goddess after she sent the plague when her bear was killed.</li>
<li>Festival of Amarysia is a celebration to worship Artemis Amarysia in <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a>. In 2007, a team of Swiss and Greek archaeologists found the ruin of Artemis Amarysia Temple, at <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>, Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>Festival of Artemis Saronia, a festival to celebrate Artemis in Trozeinos, a town in <a href="/wiki/Argolis" title="Argolis">Argolis</a>. A king named Saron built a sanctuary for the goddess after the goddess saved his life when he went hunting and was swept away by a wave. He held a festival inner honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>On the 16th day of Metageitnio (second month on the Athenian calendar), people sacrificed to Artemis and <a href="/wiki/Hecate" title="Hecate">Hecate</a> at <a href="/wiki/Deme" title="Deme">Deme</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erchia" title="Erchia">Erchia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kharis_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-kharis-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>Kharisteria Festival on 6th of Boidromion (third month) celebrates the victory of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a>, also known as the Athenian "Thanksgiving".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>Day six of Elaphobolia (ninth month) festival of Artemis the Deer Huntress where she was offered cakes shaped like stags, made from dough, honey and <a href="/wiki/Sesame" title="Sesame">sesame</a> seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li>Day 6 or 16 of Mounikhion (tenth month) is a celebration of her as the goddess of nature and animals. A goat was sacrificed to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li>Day 6 of Thargelion (eleventh month), is the Goddess's birthday, while the seventh was Apollo's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>A festival for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diktynna" title="Diktynna">Artemis Diktynna</a> (of the net) was held in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypsous" title="Hypsous">Hypsous</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laphria_(festival)" title="Laphria (festival)">Laphria</a>, a festival for Artemis in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrai" title="Patrai">Patrai</a>. The procession starts by setting logs of wood around the altar, each of them 16 <a href="/wiki/Cubit" title="Cubit">cubits</a> long. On the altar, within the circle, the driest wood is placed. Just before the festival, a smooth ascent to the altar is built by piling earth upon the altar steps. The festival begins with a splendid procession in honor of Artemis, and the maiden officiating as priestess rides last in the procession upon a chariot yoked to four deer, Artemis' traditional mode of transport (see below). However, the sacrifice is not offered until the next day.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Arcadia)" title="Orchomenus (Arcadia)">Orchomenus</a>, a sanctuary was built for Artemis Hymnia where her festival was celebrated every year.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section is empty.</b> <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(January 2013)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artemis_in_art">Artemis in art</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa,_Vis_Museum,_Croatia.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG/220px-Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG/330px-Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG/440px-Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa%2C_Vis_Museum%2C_Croatia.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_the_goddess_of_Issa,_Vis_Museum,_Croatia.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fourth century <a href="/wiki/Praxiteles" title="Praxiteles">Praxitelean</a> bronze head of a goddess wearing a <a href="/wiki/Lunate" title="Lunate">lunate</a> crown, found at Issa (<a href="/wiki/Vis_(island)" title="Vis (island)">Vis</a>, Croatia).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest representations of Artemis in Greek Archaic art portray her as <i><a href="/wiki/Potnia_Theron" title="Potnia Theron">Potnia Theron</a></i> ("Queen of the Beasts"): a winged goddess holding a stag and leopard in her hands, or sometimes a leopard and a lion. This winged Artemis lingered in ex-votos as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemis_Orthia" title="Artemis Orthia">Artemis Orthia</a>, with a sanctuary close by <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>.</p>
<p>In Greek classical art she is usually portrayed as a maiden huntress, young, tall and slim, clothed in a girl's short skirt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> with hunting boots, a quiver, a bow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and arrows. Often, she is shown in the shooting pose, and is accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog">hunting dog</a> or stag. When portrayed as a moon goddess, Artemis wore a long robe and sometimes a veil covered her head. Her darker side is revealed in some vase paintings, where she is shown as the death-bringing goddess whose arrows fell young maidens and women, such as the daughters of <a href="/wiki/Niobe" title="Niobe">Niobe</a>.</p>
<p>Artemis was sometimes represented in Classical art with the crown of the <a href="/wiki/Crescent" title="Crescent">crescent</a> <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, such as also found on <a href="/wiki/Luna_(goddess)" title="Luna (goddess)">Luna</a> and others.</p>
<p>On June 7, 2007, a Roman era bronze sculpture of <i><a href="/wiki/Artemis_and_the_Stag" title="Artemis and the Stag">Artemis and the Stag</a></i> was sold at <a href="/wiki/Sotheby%27s" title="Sotheby's">Sotheby's</a> auction house in New York state by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albright-Knox_Art_Gallery" title="Albright-Knox Art Gallery">Albright-Knox Art Gallery</a> for $25.5 million.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attributes">Attributes</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b>Bow and arrow</b></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ac_artemisephesus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ac_artemisephesus.jpg/220px-Ac_artemisephesus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ac_artemisephesus.jpg/330px-Ac_artemisephesus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ac_artemisephesus.jpg/440px-Ac_artemisephesus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ac_artemisephesus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The site of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> at Ephesus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymn</a> to Artemis, she had golden bow and arrows, as her epithet was <b>Khryselakatos</b>, "of the Golden Shaft", and <b>Iokheira</b> (Showered by Arrows). The arrows of Artemis could also bring sudden death and disease to girls and women. Artemis got her bow and arrow for the first time from The Kyklopes, as the one she asked from her father. The bow of Artemis also became the witness of Callisto's oath of her virginity. In later cult, the bow became the symbol of waxing moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Chariots</b></li>
</ul>
<p>Artemis' chariot was made of gold and was pulled by four golden horned deer (Elaphoi Khrysokeroi). The bridles of her chariot were also made of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Spears, nets, and lyre</b></li>
</ul>
<p>Although quite seldom, Artemis is sometimes portrayed with a hunting spear. Her cult in Aetolia, the Artemis Aetolian, showed her with a hunting spear. The description about Artemis' spear can be found in Ovid's Metamorphosis,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2011)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup> while Artemis with a fishing spear connected with her cult as a patron goddess of fishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a goddess of maiden dances and songs, Artemis is often portrayed with a lyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span></h4>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">Deer</a></b></li>
</ul>
<p>Deer were the only animals held sacred to Artemis herself. On seeing a deer larger than a bull with horns shining, she fell in love with these creatures and held them sacred. Deer were also the first animals she captured. She caught five golden horned deer called <b>Elaphoi Khrysokeroi</b> and harnessed them to her chariot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Labours_of_Hercules" title="Labours of Hercules">third labour of Heracles</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Eurystheus" title="Eurystheus">Eurystheus</a>, consisted in catching the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerynitian_Hind" title="Cerynitian Hind">Cerynitian Hind</a> alive. Heracles begged Artemis for forgiveness and promised to return it alive. Artemis forgave him but targeted Eurystheus for her wrath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Hunting dog</b></li>
</ul>
<p>Artemis got her hunting dogs from <a href="/wiki/Pan_(god)" title="Pan (god)">Pan</a> in the forest of Arcadia. Pan gave Artemis two black-and-white dogs, three reddish ones, and one spotted one - these dogs were able to hunt even lions. Pan also gave Artemis seven bitches of the finest Arcadian race. However, Artemis only ever brought seven dogs hunting with her at any one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">Bear</a></b></li>
</ul>
<p>The sacrifice of a bear for Artemis started with the Brauron cult. Every year a girl between five and ten years of age was sent to Artemis' temple at Brauron. The Byzantine writer Suidos relayed the legend in Arktos e Brauroniois. A bear was tamed by Artemis and introduced to the people of Athens. They touched it and played with it until one day a group of girls poked the bear until it attacked them. A brother of one of the girls killed the bear, so Artemis sent a plague in revenge. The Athenians consulted an oracle to understand how to end the plague. The oracle suggested that, in payment for the bear's blood, no Athenian virgin should be allowed to marry until she had served Artemis in her temple ('played the bear for the goddess').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boar" title="Boar">Boar</a></b></li>
</ul>
<p>The boar is one of the favorite animals of the hunters, and also hard to tame. In honor of Artemis' skill, they sacrificed it to her. Oineus and Adonis were both killed by Artemis' boar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_fowl" title="Guinea fowl">Guinea fowl</a></b></li>
</ul>
<p>Artemis felt pity for the Meleagrids as they mourned for their lost brother, Meleagor, so she transformed them into Guinea Fowl to be her favorite animals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Buzzard <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawk</a></b></li>
</ul>
<p>Hawks were the favored birds of many of the gods, Artemis included.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arecaceae" title="Arecaceae">Palm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cypress" title="Cypress">Cypress</a> were issued<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to be her birthplace. Other plants sacred to Artemis are <a href="/wiki/Amaranth" title="Amaranth">Amaranth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asphodelus" title="Asphodelus">Asphodel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artemis_as_the_Lady_of_Ephesus">Artemis as <i>the Lady of Ephesus</i></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="360" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Artemis_Ephesus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Artemis of Ephesus, 1st century AD (<a href="/wiki/Ephesus_Archaeological_Museum" title="Ephesus Archaeological Museum">Ephesus Archaeological Museum</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At Ephesus in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, Turkey, her temple became one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the World</a>. It was probably the best known center of her worship except for Delos. There the Lady whom the Ionians associated with Artemis through <i><a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">interpretatio graeca</a></i> was worshiped primarily as a <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a>, akin to the Phrygian goddess <a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Cybele</a>, in an ancient sanctuary where her <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">cult image</a> depicted the "Lady of Ephesus" adorned with multiple rounded breast-like protuberances on her chest. They have been variously interpreted as multiple <a href="/wiki/Accessory_breast" title="Accessory breast">accessory breasts</a>, as eggs, grapes, acorns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> or even bull testes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Excavation at the site of the <i>Artemision</i> in 1987-88 identified a multitude of tear-shaped <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> beads that had adorned the ancient wooden cult image or <i><a href="/wiki/Xoanon" title="Xoanon">xoanon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, Ephesian metalsmiths who felt threatened by Saint Paul's preaching of Christianity, jealously rioted in her defense, shouting ‚Äú<i>Great is Artemis of the Ephesians!</i>‚Äù<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Of the 121 columns of her temple, only one composite, made up of fragments, still stands as a marker of the temple's location. The rest were used for making churches, roads, and forts.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artemis_in_astronomy">Artemis in astronomy</span></h2>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a>, <a href="/wiki/105_Artemis" title="105 Artemis">105 Artemis</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Artemis_(crater)" title="Artemis (crater)">lunar crater</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Artemis_Chasma" title="Artemis Chasma">Artemis Chasma</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Artemis_Corona" title="Artemis Corona">Artemis Corona</a> have all been named for her.</p>
<p>Artemis is the acronym for "Architectures de bolometres pour des Telescopes a grand champ de vue dans le domaine sub-Millimetrique au Sol", a large <a href="/wiki/Bolometer" title="Bolometer">bolometer</a> camera in the <a href="/wiki/Submillimetre_astronomy" title="Submillimetre astronomy">submillimeter</a> range that was installed in 2010 at the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Pathfinder_Experiment" title="Atacama Pathfinder Experiment">Atacama Pathfinder Experiment</a> (APEX), located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atacama_desert" title="Atacama desert">Atacama Desert</a> in northern Chile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artemis_in_taxonomy">Artemis in taxonomy</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemia" title="Artemia">Artemia</a></i>, which entirely comprises the family Artemiidae, derives from Artemis. <i>Artemia</i> are aquatic crustaceans known as brine shrimp, the best known species of which, <i><a href="/wiki/Artemia_salina" title="Artemia salina">Artemia salina</a></i>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_Monkeys" title="Sea Monkeys">Sea Monkeys</a>, was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> in 1758. <i>Artemia</i> live in salt lakes, and although they are almost never found in an open sea, they do appear along the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean coast</a> near <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> once stood.</p>

<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Greek poets could not decide whether her bow was silver or gold: "Over the shadowy hills and windy peaks she draws her golden bow." (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymn</a> to Artemis), and it is a golden bow as well in <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> 1.693, where her nymph's is of horn. "And how often goddess, didst thou make trial of thy silver bow?", asks <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a> for whom it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cydonia_(ancient_Greece)" title="Cydonia (ancient Greece)">Cydonian</a> bow that the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a> make for her (Callimachus, Hymn 3 to Artemis).</span></li>

(eds.) <i>From Artemis to Diana: the goddess of man and beast</i>. Collegium Hyperboreum and Museum Tusculanum Press, Copenhagen, 2009</a></li>
